     <p>
      <i>
       <b>
        Emotions
       </b>
      </i>
      is the second
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Studio_album" title="Studio album">
       studio album
      </a>
      by American
      <a href="/wiki/Singer-songwriter" title="Singer-songwriter">
       singer-songwriter
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Mariah_Carey" title="Mariah Carey">
       Mariah Carey
      </a>
      . It was released on September 17, 1991 by
      <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Records" title="Columbia Records">
       Columbia Records
      </a>
      . The album deviated from the formula of Carey's
      <a href="/wiki/Mariah_Carey_(album)" title="Mariah Carey (album)">
       1990 self-titled debut album
      </a>
      , as she had more creative control over the material she produced and recorded. Additionally,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      features influences from a range of genres such as
      <a href="/wiki/Gospel_music" title="Gospel music">
       gospel
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_R%26B" title="Contemporary R&amp;B">
       R&amp;B
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">
       soul
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">
       pop
      </a>
      and 1950s, 1960s and 1970s balladry infusion. On the record, Carey worked with a variety of producers and writers, including
      <a href="/wiki/Walter_Afanasieff" title="Walter Afanasieff">
       Walter Afanasieff
      </a>
      , the only hold over from her previous effort. Additionally, Carey wrote and produced the album's material with
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Clivill%C3%A9s" title="Robert Clivills">
       Robert Clivills
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Cole_(producer)" title="David Cole (producer)">
       David Cole
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/C%2BC_Music_Factory" title="C+C Music Factory">
       C+C Music Factory
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Carole_King" title="Carole King">
       Carole King
      </a>
      , with whom she wrote one song.
     </p>
     <p>
      Upon release,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      received generally mixed reviews from contemporary music critics. The album debuted at number four on the
      <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200">
       <i>
        Billboard
       </i>
       200
      </a>
      , surprising many critics following the success of Carey's debut, which spent eleven weeks atop the chart. While selling far less than
      <i>
       Mariah Carey
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      was eventually certified quadruple-
      <a href="/wiki/RIAA_certification" title="RIAA certification">
       platinum
      </a>
      by the
      <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">
       Recording Industry Association of America
      </a>
      (RIAA), denoting shipments of four million copies throughout the country, with estimated sales standing at 3,595,000 copies.
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      achieved moderate success outside the United States, peaking within the top ten in Australia, Canada, the Netherlands, New Zealand, Norway and the United Kingdom. Its success in Japan was strong, shipping one million copies there. The album has sold 8 million copies worldwide.
     </p>
     <p>
      Three commercial singles were released from the album.
      <a href="/wiki/Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Emotions (Mariah Carey song)">
       The title track
      </a>
      , the album's lead song, became Carey's fifth chart topper on the
      <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100">
       <i>
        Billboard
       </i>
       Hot 100
      </a>
      , making her the only artist in history to have their first five singles reach the chart's summit. Additionally, it became Carey's third chart topper in Canada, and reached the top ten in France, the Netherlands and New Zealand. "
      <a href="/wiki/Can%27t_Let_Go_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Can't Let Go (Mariah Carey song)">
       Can't Let Go
      </a>
      " was released as the second single from
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      on October 23, 1991. Due to Columbia's removal of the single from stores in an attempt to boost the album's sales, "Can't Let Go" failed to become her sixth chart topper in the US, peaking at number two. European and worldwide success was very limited, reaching the top 20 in only Canada and the UK. Similarly, "
      <a href="/wiki/Make_It_Happen_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Make It Happen (Mariah Carey song)">
       Make It Happen
      </a>
      " peaked at number five in the US, and achieved relatively weak international charting, prompting Columbia to halt promotion of the album.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          Charts and certifications
         </span>
        </a>
        <ul>
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12">
          <a href="#Charts">
           <span class="tocnumber">
            11.1
           </span>
           <span class="toctext">
            Charts
           </span>
          </a>
         </li>
         <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13">
          <a href="#Certifications">
           <span class="tocnumber">
            11.2
           </span>
           <span class="toctext">
            Certifications
           </span>
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14">
        <a href="#References">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          12
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          References
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15">
        <a href="#Works_cited">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          13
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          Works cited
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
     </div>
     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Background">
       Background
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Following the success of Carey's
      <a href="/wiki/Mariah_Carey_(album)" title="Mariah Carey (album)">
       self-titled
      </a>
      debut album, critics wondered whether or not she would tour in order to promote the album in the major worldwide music markets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, Carey expressed in several interviews that due to the strenuous nature and the sheer difficulty of her songs, she feared a tour with back-to-back shows would not be possible, aside from the long travel times and constant travel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With the extra time, Carey began writing and producing material for
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      around the same time that her debut's third single, "
      <a href="/wiki/Someday_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Someday (Mariah Carey song)">
       Someday
      </a>
      ", was released in December 1990. During this time period in music, it was traditional for an artist to release a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Studio_album" title="Studio album">
       studio album
      </a>
      every two years in their prime, allowing the singles to fully promote the album through airwaves, as well as television appearances.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson2_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson2-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Additionally, after a tour that would usually follow, as the next album would be released and would gain new fans, they would search the artist's catalog, and purchase the previous album in hopes of learning of their older work.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson2_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson2-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Sony_Music_Entertainment" title="Sony Music Entertainment">
       Sony
      </a>
      , however, chose to market Carey in a different fashion, leaning towards the traditional form in the 1960s, where acts would release an LP every year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson3_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson3-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They felt that Carey's reputation of being a "studio worm" and a songwriter from a young age would be captivating enough to deliver a new album more often than most.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson3_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson3-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As writing for the album came under way, Carey had a falling out with
      <a href="/wiki/Ben_Margulies" title="Ben Margulies">
       Ben Margulies
      </a>
      , the man whom Carey had written seven of the eleven songs on Carey's debut with.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson3_8-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson3-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Together, the duo had written and produced seven songs for Carey's demo tape which she handed to
      <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Mottola" title="Tommy Mottola">
       Tommy Mottola
      </a>
      . Their parting of ways was due to a contract Carey had signed prior to her signing with
      <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Records" title="Columbia Records">
       Columbia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson3_8-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson3-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Carey had agreed to split not only the songwriting royalties from the songs, but half of her earnings as well, something she never thought twice about while writing songs in his father's basement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson3_8-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson3-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, when the time came to write music for
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      , Sony officials made it clear he would only be paid the fair amount given to co-writers on an album.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson3_8-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson3-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following the discussion, Margulies filed a lawsuit against Sony, claiming that under contract, he would be entitled to work with Carey, as well as reap extra benefits. After an almost one year lawsuit, the judge settled that Margulies was to earn ten percent of Carey's direct earnings from her record sales, not including an income from any other ventures.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson3_8-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson3-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While settled, their relationship remained ruined, damaged by what Carey considered treachery. In an interview with
      <a href="/wiki/Fred_Bronson" title="Fred Bronson">
       Fred Bronson
      </a>
      , Carey said the following regarding the contract: "I signed blindly. Later, I tried to make it right so we could continue...but he wouldn't accept it."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson3_8-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson3-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After the settlement, Margulies spoke of his feelings on the matter, claiming he would hope to one day write again with Carey, placing most of the blame on the record label and concluding "Hopefully one day, art will prevail over business."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson3_8-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson3-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Recording">
       Recording
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Recording">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <i>
       Mariah Carey
      </i>
      had originally been recorded in Margulies' father's basement, with old and minimal equipment. After being signed to Columbia, the songs that would be used for the album were re-mastered and re-recorded in professional studios.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson4_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson4-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, due to Sony's involvement in the project, they did not allow Carey to produce most of the album, hoping the aid of several famed record producers would be able to ensure Carey's already deemed "exquisite" songs would become popular. After the album's success however, Carey was allowed more freedom on
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      than on her debut.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson4_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson4-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since she no longer had a working or personal relationship with Margulies, she chose to work with mostly different musicians than those of her previous effort, with the exception of
      <a href="/wiki/Walter_Afanasieff" title="Walter Afanasieff">
       Walter Afanasieff
      </a>
      , the only hold over from
      <i>
       Mariah Carey
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson4_9-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson4-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Even though he had only co-written "
      <a href="/wiki/Love_Takes_Time" title="Love Takes Time">
       Love Takes Time
      </a>
      ", and had only produced part of the album, Carey felt a strong working chemistry with him, soon developing a unique form of songwriting alongside him. Aside from Afanasieff, Carey worked with
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Clivill%C3%A9s" title="Robert Clivills">
       Robert Clivills
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Cole_(producer)" title="David Cole (producer)">
       David Cole
      </a>
      from the dance-music influenced production duo,
      <a href="/wiki/C%2BC_Music_Factory" title="C+C Music Factory">
       C+C Music Factory
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson4_9-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson4-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Working with the duo was originally Mottola's suggestion, but after meeting the pair, Carey agreed and wrote four songs together with them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson4_9-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson4-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Additionally, aside from the three men, Carey worked with
      <a href="/wiki/Carole_King" title="Carole King">
       Carole King
      </a>
      , a female
      <a href="/wiki/Singer-songwriter" title="Singer-songwriter">
       singer-songwriter
      </a>
      who had been predominantly popular in the 1970s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson5_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson5-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, unlike with C+C Music Factory, King approached Carey, hoping to work with her after hearing her perform live on
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Arsenio_Hall_Show" title="The Arsenio Hall Show">
        The Arsenio Hall Show
       </a>
      </i>
      . During a conversation with Carey, King suggested that she cover "
      <a href="/wiki/(You_Make_Me_Feel_Like)_A_Natural_Woman" title="(You Make Me Feel Like) A Natural Woman">
       (You Make Me Feel Like) A Natural Woman
      </a>
      ", a song she had written with
      <a href="/wiki/Gerry_Goffin" title="Gerry Goffin">
       Gerry Goffin
      </a>
      for
      <a href="/wiki/Aretha_Franklin" title="Aretha Franklin">
       Aretha Franklin
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson5_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson5-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After giving it some thought, Carey declined the offer, feeling uncomfortable about recording a song she felt one of her musical influences performed so perfectly.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson5_10-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson5-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Still determined on working with Carey, King flew to New York for one day, to try to create a ballad of some sort.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson5_10-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson5-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The two ladies sat together by a piano over the course of the day, and by nightfall, had written and arranged a song titled "
      <a href="/wiki/If_It%27s_Over" title="If It's Over">
       If It's Over
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson6_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson6-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After working with Carey, King said in an interview "I love her voice. She's very expressive. She gives a lot of meaning to what she sings."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson6_11-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson6-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After recording "If It's Over", Carey expressed the musical connection she shared with Afanasieff, as well as the creative format in which she wrote and produced her music when with him, or working with C+C Music Factory.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson7_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson7-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When working with Afanasieff, the duo would sit by a piano, and lead each other vocally and musically, until they would reach the right note and arrangement. During an interview in 1992, Afanasieff described how Carey would stand next to him, and begin singing different notes and tunes she was thinking of, while he would follow her with the piano.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson7_12-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson7-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In doing so, he would help lead her to the right note and vice versa. Carey described their working relationship as "very unique," and felt it to be very similar to the form in which she had worked with Margulies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson7_12-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson7-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While similar, Carey's creative process with Cole and Clivills proved different; they would bring her several different tapes and tunes, of which she would choose from. Afterwards, they would work on building the already created melody, and have Carey add and build onto it, as well as writing the lyrics and
      <a href="/wiki/Key_(music)" title="Key (music)">
       key
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson7_12-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson7-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Music_and_lyrics">
       Music and lyrics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Music and lyrics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div class="center">

         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">

      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <span>
         <i>
          Problems playing these files? See
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">
           media help
          </a>
          .
         </i>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Unlike Carey's debut album, which featured a more contemporary
      <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">
       pop
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_R%26B" title="Contemporary R&amp;B">
       R&amp;B
      </a>
      background,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      proved to be far different. It borrowed from several genres ranging from gospel,
      <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_R%26B" title="Contemporary R&amp;B">
       R&amp;B
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">
       soul
      </a>
      , pop and 1960s and 1970s influences.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nick5_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nick5-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson8_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson8-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The album's lead single "
      <a href="/wiki/Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Emotions (Mariah Carey song)">
       Emotions
      </a>
      " borrowed heavily from 1970s disco, and flaunted Carey's
      <a href="/wiki/Vocal_range" title="Vocal range">
       upper range
      </a>
      and extensive use of the
      <a href="/wiki/Whistle_register" title="Whistle register">
       whistle register
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson8_14-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson8-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The song's lyrics were described as "joyful" by author Chris Nickson, and told of a strong and deep emotion felt by the protagonist when with their lover.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson8_14-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson8-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One of the album's more gospel infused songs, "And You Don't Remember", featured organ
      <a href="/wiki/Chord_progression" title="Chord progression">
       chord changes
      </a>
      and held minimal production in order to give the
      <a href="/wiki/Singing" title="Singing">
       vocals
      </a>
      a more "raw and sixties feel."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson8_14-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson8-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It and the former song were part of a trio of tracks from the album that were meant to pay homage to
      <a href="/wiki/Motown" title="Motown">
       Motown
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Ballad" title="Ballad">
       ballads
      </a>
      , with the inclusion of soft church
      <a href="/wiki/Choir" title="Choir">
       choir
      </a>
      vocals, and sole
      <a href="/wiki/Arrangement" title="Arrangement">
       musical arrangement
      </a>
      by Carey.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson8_14-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson8-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Its
      <a href="/wiki/Lyrics" title="Lyrics">
       lyrics
      </a>
      reflected the song's raw chorus, telling of girl that is promised the world by her boyfriend, and quickly forget about her and moves to the next one. After the heartbreak, the protagonist asks him "Don't You Remember" all those things he had promised her, and the things they had spoken and dreamed about doing together.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson8_14-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson8-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "
      <a href="/wiki/Can%27t_Let_Go_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Can't Let Go (Mariah Carey song)">
       Can't Let Go
      </a>
      ", the album's second single, is a slow ballad, featuring sad and yearning lyrical content.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson9_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson9-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The song's introduction featured minor chord changes, and drew influence from
      <a href="/wiki/1950s_in_music" title="1950s in music">
       fifties balladry
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson9_15-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson9-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For the duration of the first half of the song, Carey sings in her lower and huskier registers, eventually leading to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Belt_(music)" title="Belt (music)">
       belted
      </a>
      crescendo and
      <a href="/wiki/Falsetto" title="Falsetto">
       falsetto
      </a>
      and whistle finish.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson9_15-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson9-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Of the ten tracks on the album, Carey felt her most autobiographical lyrics were featured on "
      <a href="/wiki/Make_It_Happen_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Make It Happen (Mariah Carey song)">
       Make It Happen
      </a>
      ", which told of Carey's poor and difficult teen life prior to being signed by Columbia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson9_15-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson9-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It continues telling of the importance of faith and prayer to God. Nickson described its instrumentation as "restrained" and "very Motownish," as well as noting its soft gospel infusion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson9_15-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson9-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Critically, the most anticipated song on the album was Carey's collaboration with King. It was influenced by sixties and seventies gospel and other soulful genres.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson10_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson10-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to Nickson, the song's instrumentation and basis was crucial to Carey's performance throughout the song. Additionally, he described its content and instrumentation:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       As a song full of gospel and soulful influences, it allowed Mariah to really tear loose and show what she could do  which in reality was far more than the vocal gymnastics that seemed to comprise her reputation so far. From a deep rumble to a high wail, she covered five octaves wonderfully, as the power of the tune built. The backing vocals  which once again had those churchy harmonies  filled out the spare melody, as did the stately horns, which entered towards the end. The song was truly a vocal showcase for Mariah.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson10_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nickson10-16">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         16
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The next song on the album's track list, "You're So Cold", was originally intended to be the lead single from
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      , eventually being switched for the title track. The song's introduction features a
      <a href="/wiki/Piano" title="Piano">
       piano
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">
       a capella
      </a>
      vocal, working into its chorus.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson10_16-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson10-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Chris Nickson wrote "The song sailed into the chorus, driven by the house-y piano work, the bubbly, snacking rhythm belying the angry lyrics, the upbeat tone of voice."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson10_16-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson10-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As Nickson hinted at, its lyrics featured an angry message, calling out an unfaithful lover and asking how he could be "So Cold." "So Blessed" was a song Carey wrote with Afanasieff, infusing fifties style pop balladry into it. Carey's voice in the song is very restrained, as she stays within her lower registers throughout the duration of the track.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson10_16-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson10-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "To Be Around You" was described by Nickson as "far more
      <a href="/wiki/Staccato" title="Staccato">
       staccato
      </a>
      ." Its production and melody was intended to pay tribute to "
      <a href="/wiki/Got_to_Be_Real" title="Got to Be Real">
       Got to Be Real
      </a>
      " by
      <a href="/wiki/Cheryl_Lynn" title="Cheryl Lynn">
       Cheryl Lynn
      </a>
      , as well as featuring spoken voices towards the end of the song.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson10_16-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson10-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nickson described "Till the End of Time" as a "gentle, almost lullaby melody."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson11_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson11-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was a love ballad, preparing the listener for the song's final track, "The Wind". The latter song featured the album's strongest
      <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">
       jazz
      </a>
      influence, and sampled a piano melody from
      <a href="/wiki/Russ_Freeman_(pianist)" title="Russ Freeman (pianist)">
       Russell Freeman
      </a>
      during the 1950s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson11_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson11-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After Afanasieff presented Carey with the melody he had discovered, it inspired her to write the
      <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">
       melody
      </a>
      and lyrics, which told of a friend that perished in a drunk-driving accident.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson11_17-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson11-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Musically, the album fulfilled its greatest challenge, according to critics. It had helped master Carey's usage and infusion of several genres which she had not tapped into during the recording of her debut.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nickson11_17-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nickson11-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">
       Critical reception
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Critical reception">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>

        </span>

          <span>
           ]

         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>

      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle">
        <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">
         Robert Christgau
        </a>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle">
        <span style="white-space:nowrap" title="(dud)">
         <img alt="(dud)" data-file-height="207" data-file-width="265" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Rating-Christgau-dud.svg/17px-Rating-Christgau-dud.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Rating-Christgau-dud.svg/26px-Rating-Christgau-dud.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Rating-Christgau-dud.svg/34px-Rating-Christgau-dud.svg.png 2x" title="(dud)" width="17"/>
        </span>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christgau_25-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-christgau-25">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          25
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle">
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">
          Rolling Stone
         </a>
        </i>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle">
        <span style="white-space:nowrap" title="2/5 stars">
         <img alt="2/5 stars" data-file-height="110" data-file-width="108" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/11px-Star_full.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/17px-Star_full.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/22px-Star_full.svg.png 2x" title="2/5 stars" width="11"/>
         <img alt="" data-file-height="110" data-file-width="108" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/11px-Star_full.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/17px-Star_full.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/22px-Star_full.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
         <img alt="" data-file-height="110" data-file-width="108" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/11px-Star_empty.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/17px-Star_empty.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/22px-Star_empty.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
         <img alt="" data-file-height="110" data-file-width="108" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/11px-Star_empty.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/17px-Star_empty.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/22px-Star_empty.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
         <img alt="" data-file-height="110" data-file-width="108" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/11px-Star_empty.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/17px-Star_empty.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_empty.svg/22px-Star_empty.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
        </span>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tannen_26-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-tannen-26">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          26
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle">
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Florida_Sun-Sentinel" title="South Florida Sun-Sentinel">
          Sun-Sentinel
         </a>
        </i>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle">
        (Mixed)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilker_27-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-wilker-27">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          27
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Upon its release,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      garnered generally mixed to positive reviews from contemporary music critics, many of whom both praised and criticized the album's content alongside Carey's vocal acrobatics. Bill Lamb from
      <a href="/wiki/About.com" title="About.com">
       About.com
      </a>
      gave
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      three and a half out of five stars, complimenting the album as a whole, claiming it to be "tightened" when in comparison to the original. He described the album's lead single as "bone chilling," however criticizing some of the Carey's high notes as "vocal range overkill."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lamb_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-lamb-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allmusic" title="Allmusic">
       Allmusic
      </a>
      editor, Ashley Battel, gave the album four out of five stars, calling it a "musical journey." Additionally, Erlewine picked "
      <a href="/wiki/Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Emotions (Mariah Carey song)">
       Emotions
      </a>
      " and "
      <a href="/wiki/Make_It_Happen_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Make It Happen (Mariah Carey song)">
       Make It Happen
      </a>
      " as the album's standout tracks and wrote "The one emotion that prevails upon completion of the album is definitely a positive one: satisfaction."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erlewine_19-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-erlewine-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Parry Gettelman, editor from the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Orlando_Sentinel" title="Orlando Sentinel">
        Orlando Sentinel
       </a>
      </i>
      , was critical on Carey's vocal acrobatics, writing "Carey has become so enamored of the ultra-high-frequency part of her range that I'm starting to suspect she may be an intergalactic spy trying to re- establish communications with the far-off Planet of Dogs."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gettel_23-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-gettel-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jonathan Kurant from the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Florida_Sun-Sentinel" title="South Florida Sun-Sentinel">
        Sun-Sentinel
       </a>
      </i>
      gave
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      a mixed review, writing "Oddly, the album gets more original at the end, where not all people will bother getting to." Further in the review, he outed "The Wind" and "Till the End of Time" as the album's stand out tracks, but felt most of the album was unoriginal and not an improvement over her debut.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kurant_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-kurant-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Steve Morse from
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Boston_Globe" title="The Boston Globe">
        The Boston Globe
       </a>
      </i>
      gave the album a positive review, calling it "a quantum leap in maturity and confidence." Morse felt
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      was superior to Carey's debut, calling its lyrics "remarkable," its ballads "unspeakably beautiful," and Carey's vocal and songwriting ability "unlimited."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morse_20-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-morse-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rob Tannenbaum from
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">
        Rolling Stone
       </a>
      </i>
      was critical on the album, expressing how Carey's extensive use of her range made it difficult to truly feel and connect to the lyrics within the songs. Tannenbaum concluded his review on
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      with "Carey has a remarkable vocal gift, but to date, unfortunately, her singing has been far more impressive than expressive."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tannen_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-tannen-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Arion Berger from
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Weekly" title="Entertainment Weekly">
        Entertainment Weekly
       </a>
      </i>
      gave the album a C, calling it "colder and more calculated" than Carey's debut. Additionally, Berger mirrored similar sentiments written by Tannenbaum, writing "[Emotions] is the hybrid progeny of a venerable tradition  the tradition of the R&amp;B diva  and crass commercial instincts. it's gospel without soul, love songs without passion, pop without buoyancy."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berger_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-berger-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">
       Robert Christgau
      </a>
      said nothing on the album, but gave it a "dud" score.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christgau_25-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-christgau-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Deborah Wilker, editor of the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Florida_Sun-Sentinel" title="South Florida Sun-Sentinel">
        Sun-Sentinel
       </a>
      </i>
      , complimented Carey's vocal and singing abilities, however criticizing some of the album's content as a whole. Wilker wrote "With her elite industry connections and top-notch voice, Carey can do better. Sure this set is enjoyable and provides pop fans with a new diva to idolize, but Carey and company don`t come close to exploiting the full range of her musical potential."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilker_27-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-wilker-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dennis Hunt from the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">
        Los Angeles Times
       </a>
      </i>
      gave the album two out of a possible four stars. After comparing calling Carey's voice to that of
      <a href="/wiki/Whitney_Houston" title="Whitney Houston">
       Whitney Houston
      </a>
      's, he called it "spectacular and impressive," but criticized the album's songs and production for "playing high on the angst scale."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hunt_24-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-hunt-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Editor of the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">
        Chicago Tribune
       </a>
      </i>
      , Jan DeKnock, gave the album three stars, indicating a "Good" review. She called some of the album's ballads "boring," but described Carey's voice as "breathtaking."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deknock_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-deknock-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_performance">
       Commercial performance
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Commercial performance">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey_in_August_2006_4.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Mariah_Carey_in_August_2006_4.jpg/170px-Mariah_Carey_in_August_2006_4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Mariah_Carey_in_August_2006_4.jpg/255px-Mariah_Carey_in_August_2006_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Mariah_Carey_in_August_2006_4.jpg/340px-Mariah_Carey_in_August_2006_4.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mariah_Carey_in_August_2006_4.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Carey performing "
        <a href="/wiki/Make_It_Happen_(Mariah_Carey_song)" title="Make It Happen (Mariah Carey song)">
         Make It Happen
        </a>
        " in Florida, during her
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Mimi_Tour" title="The Adventures of Mimi Tour">
         Adventures of Mimi Tour
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      debuted at the number four position on the
      <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200">
       <i>
        Billboard
       </i>
       200
      </a>
      , with first-week sales of 129,000 copies, surprising critics following the success of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Mariah_Carey_(album)" title="Mariah Carey (album)">
        Mariah Carey
       </a>
      </i>
      (1990). In total, the album spent twenty-seven weeks in the top twenty and a total of fifty-five on the albums chart, becoming Carey's lowest-peaking album until
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Glitter_(soundtrack)" title="Glitter (soundtrack)">
        Glitter
       </a>
      </i>
      (2001).
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      was certified quadruple-
      <a href="/wiki/RIAA_certification" title="RIAA certification">
       platinum
      </a>
      by the
      <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">
       Recording Industry Association of America
      </a>
      (RIAA), denoting shipments of four million copies within the United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riaa_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-riaa-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to
      <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_SoundScan" title="Nielsen SoundScan">
       Nielsen SoundScan
      </a>
      , the album's stateside sales are estimated at 3,595,000.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On the
      <a href="/wiki/RPM_(magazine)" title="RPM (magazine)">
       Canadian
       <i>
        RPM
       </i>
       Albums Chart
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      debuted at number fourteen, on the issue dated October 5, 1991.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Four weeks later, on the issue date November 2, 1991, the album reached its peak position of number six, staying there for one week.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-can_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-can-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the end of the year,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      finished number 35 on the Year-End Albums Chart of 1991.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canend_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-canend-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      To date, the album has been certified quadruple-
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_music_recording_certifications" title="List of music recording certifications">
       platinum
      </a>
      by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_Recording_Industry_Association" title="Canadian Recording Industry Association">
       Canadian Recording Industry Association
      </a>
      (CRIA), denoting shipments of 400,000 units.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canadian_Certification_database_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Canadian_Certification_database-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Japan,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      debuted at number three on the official
      <a href="/wiki/Oricon" title="Oricon">
       Oricon
      </a>
      chart, and according to
      <a href="/wiki/Sony_Music_Entertainment" title="Sony Music Entertainment">
       Sony Music
      </a>
      , has sold 1,000,000 copies throughout the country.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jap_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-jap-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sales_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sales-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Australia, the album debuted at number twelve on the
      <a href="/wiki/ARIA_Charts" title="ARIA Charts">
       Australian Albums Chart
      </a>
      during the week of October 10, 1991, attaining its peak position of number eight the following week.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aus_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-aus-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After eighteen weeks fluctuating within the chart,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      dropped out of the top forty, being certified platinum by the
      <a href="/wiki/Australian_Recording_Industry_Association" title="Australian Recording Industry Association">
       Australian Recording Industry Association
      </a>
      (ARIA).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aus_37-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-aus-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kent_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kent-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In France,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      received a gold certifications from the
      <a href="/wiki/Syndicat_National_de_l%27%C3%89dition_Phonographique" title="Syndicat National de l'dition Phonographique">
       Syndicat National de l'dition Phonographique
      </a>
      (SNEP), denoting shipments of 100,000 units.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snep_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-snep-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The album made its debut on the
      <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Top_40" title="Dutch Top 40">
       Dutch Top 40
      </a>
      at number seventy-nine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neth_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-neth-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The following week, it moved up to number fifty-nine, which became its peak charting position.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neth_40-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-neth-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In total,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      spent six weeks within the Dutch charts, being certified platinum by the
      <a href="/wiki/NVPI" title="NVPI">
       Nederlandse Vereniging van Producenten en Importeurs van beeld- en geluidsdragers
      </a>
      (NVPI), denoting shipments of 100,000 units.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nether_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nether-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During the week of October 17, 1991,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      debuted at its peak position of number six, spending a total of sixteen weeks on the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_New_Zealand" title="Recording Industry Association of New Zealand">
       New Zealand Albums Chart
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-new-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_New_Zealand" title="Recording Industry Association of New Zealand">
       Recording Industry Association of New Zealand
      </a>
      (RIANZ) certified the album platinum, denoting shipments of 15,000 units within the country.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scapolo_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-scapolo-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Sweden,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      debuted at number twenty-six on the
      <a href="/wiki/Sverigetopplistan" title="Sverigetopplistan">
       Swedish Albums Chart
      </a>
      , peaking at number thirteen and spending a total of five weeks fluctuating in the chart.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swe_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-swe-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following its exit from the chart, the album was certified platinum by the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_the_Phonographic_Industry" title="International Federation of the Phonographic Industry">
       International Federation of the Phonographic Industry
      </a>
      (IFPI), denoting shipments of 100,000 units.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swedish_Certifications_1987-1998_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Swedish_Certifications_1987-1998-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On October 13, 1991,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      debuted at number sixteen on the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiss_Music_Charts" title="Swiss Music Charts">
       Swiss Albums Chart
      </a>
      , attaining its peak position of fifteen the succeeding week.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swi_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-swi-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following a run of nine weeks in the albums chart, the album was certified gold by the IFPI, denoting shipments of 50,000 units throughout the country.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swiss_Certifications_1991_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Swiss_Certifications_1991-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On the
      <a href="/wiki/UK_Albums_Chart" title="UK Albums Chart">
       UK Albums Chart
      </a>
      , dated October 26, 1991, the album debuted at number ten.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In its seventeenth week,
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      attained its peak position of number four, placing higher than Carey's debut reach of six.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Top_40_Official_UK_Singles_Archive_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Top_40_Official_UK_Singles_Archive-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After charting in the United Kingdom for forty weeks, the album was certified platinum by the
      <a href="/wiki/British_Phonographic_Industry" title="British Phonographic Industry">
       British Phonographic Industry
      </a>
      (BPI), denoting shipments of 300,000 units.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bpi_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bpi-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       Emotions
      </i>
      has sold eight million copies worldwide, short of the 15 million sold by her debut in 1990.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sales_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-sales-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bet_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bet-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Singles">
       Singles
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_album)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Singles">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div class="center">
         <div class="floatnone">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <div class="haudio">
         <div style="padding:4px 0">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mariah_Carry_-_Emotions.ogg" title="File:Mariah Carry - Emotions.ogg">
           "Emotions"
          </a>
         </div>
         <div>
          <div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px">
           <audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="23.613922902494" data-mwprovider="local" data-mwtitle="Emotions_(Mariah_Carey_song_-_sample).ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_2" poster="//en.wikipedia.org/static/1.26wmf13/resources/assets/file-type-icons/fileicon-ogg.png" preload="none" style="width:220px">
